The center passed just west of Downtown <a href="/wiki/Shreveport" class="mw-redirect" title="Shreveport">Shreveport</a> before crossing the <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a> border. At the height of the storm, over 175,000 people had lost power in the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> Shreveport\'s forecast area, mainly across <a href="/wiki/Deep_East_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Deep East Texas">Deep East Texas</a> into northwest Louisiana. Two fatalities occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Ark-La-Tex" title="Ark-La-Tex">Ark-La-Tex</a>. A tree fell on one person; the other fatality occurred when a teenager was electrocuted when picking up a "hot" power line. Shreveport recorded its 2nd lowest pressure ever recorded as the center of Rita moved through Shreveport around 6 pm Saturday evening. The pressure recorded was 29.05 inches of mercury (984&#160;mb) which was only .01&#160;inch higher than the lowest pressure on record of 29.04&#160;inches back on February 27, 1902.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mississippi">Mississippi</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mississippi">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Mississippi" title="Mississippi">Mississippi</a>, Rita produced widespread rainfall upon its landfall in Louisiana;<sup id="cite_ref-HPC_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HPC-93">[93]</a></sup> however, most of the rain fell early on September&#160;25 as a band of heavy rain developed over parts of western Mississippi, northeast Louisiana and southern Arkansas, resulting in up to 10&#160;in (250&#160;mm) of rain around the <a href="/wiki/Big_Black_River_(Mississippi)" title="Big Black River (Mississippi)">Big Black River</a><sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_Miss._94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC_Miss.-94">[94]</a></sup> in the span of a few hours.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The heavy rainfall caused significant flooding in <a href="/wiki/Yazoo_County,_Mississippi" title="Yazoo County, Mississippi">Yazoo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warren_County,_Mississippi" title="Warren County, Mississippi">Warren</a> Counties. In Yazoo, numerous homes had water inside and countywide damage amounted to $6&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Damage in Warren County was less than Yazoo, amounting to $2.7&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Holmes_County,_Mississippi" title="Holmes County, Mississippi">Holmes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinds_County,_Mississippi" title="Hinds County, Mississippi">Hinds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Madison_County,_Mississippi" title="Madison County, Mississippi">Madison</a> Counties also had flooding, with damage in all three counties amounting to $2&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Several roads were also flooded in <a href="/wiki/Monroe_County,_Mississippi" title="Monroe County, Mississippi">Monroe County</a> after 6 inches (150&#160;mm) of rain fell.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Winds up to 70&#160;mph (110&#160;km/h) downed numerous trees throughout the state.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC-102">[102]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Adams_County,_Mississippi" title="Adams County, Mississippi">Adams County</a>, winds caused several trees to fall on homes in <a href="/wiki/Natchez,_Mississippi" title="Natchez, Mississippi">Natchez</a>, leaving $270,000 in damage.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In Warren County, a mobile home was destroyed after a tree was downed by high winds.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>\n<p>An unusually large amount of tornadoes touched down in the state due to Rita, with 54 confirmed in Mississippi alone. The size of the <a href="/wiki/Tornado_outbreak" title="Tornado outbreak">tornado outbreak</a> ranked it as the largest recorded by the <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> office in <a href="/wiki/Jackson,_Mississippi" title="Jackson, Mississippi">Jackson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_Miss._94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC_Miss.-94">[94]</a></sup> An F1 tornado killed one person after tossing a mobile home into the air and destroying it, two other occupants sustained serious injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Damage from tornadoes alone in the state amounted to $14.5&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC-102">[102]</a></sup> Six F2 tornadoes touched down in Mississippi,<sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC-102">[102]</a></sup> one of these tracked for nearly 18 miles (29&#160;km) and grew to a width of 800&#160;yd (730&#160;m). The tornado caused $2.5&#160;million in damage and injured three people after destroying one building and severely damaging several homes and farms.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Another F1 tornado struck a mobile home park, destroying eleven homes, injuring seven people and leaving $2&#160;million in damages.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Throughout the state, 2,127 residences lost power due to high winds.<sup id="cite_ref-PowerRpt4_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PowerRpt4-87">[87]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Texas_2">Texas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Texas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-image">\n<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>\n</td>\n<td class="mbox-text">\n<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(September 2017)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg/220px-HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg" width="220" height="292" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg/330px-HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg/440px-HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg 2x" data-file-width="540" data-file-height="716" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HurricaneRitaFlooding_AMO_20050925.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nTwo satellite images showing the extent of flooding caused by Rita in Louisiana and Texas.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The impact of Rita, limited to Southeast and East Texas, varied, with both wind and storm-surge damage impacting communities in various ways. Hurricane-force winds extended over 100 miles (160&#160;km) inland across eastern Texas, causing extensive damage to trees in the <a href="/wiki/Piney_Woods" title="Piney Woods">Piney Woods</a>. Communities near and along the west side of the Sabine river, from the Gulf coast up to <a href="/wiki/Toledo_Bend_Reservoir" title="Toledo Bend Reservoir">Toledo Bend Reservoir</a>, saw the greatest measure of damage. The worst damage in the state occurred in several counties in Southeast Texas, including <a href="/wiki/Jefferson_County,_Texas" title="Jefferson County, Texas">Jefferson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orange_County,_Texas" title="Orange County, Texas">Orange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hardin_County,_Texas" title="Hardin County, Texas">Hardin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jasper_County,_Texas" title="Jasper County, Texas">Jasper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Newton_County,_Texas" title="Newton County, Texas">Newton</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tyler_County,_Texas" title="Tyler County, Texas">Tyler</a> counties, where damage to electrical and communication services was severe. Power was not restored in some areas across the region for weeks to even months. Cities in the <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Texas#Golden_Triangle" title="Southeast Texas">"Golden Triangle"</a> formed by <a href="/wiki/Beaumont,_Texas" title="Beaumont, Texas">Beaumont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Port_Arthur,_Texas" title="Port Arthur, Texas">Port Arthur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Orange,_TX" class="mw-redirect" title="Orange, TX">Orange, TX</a> sustained extensive wind damage, whether directly from wind, or collaterally from wind-fallen trees, tree branches and/or other detritus. In Beaumont, an estimated 25% of the trees in neighborhoods across the city were uprooted or heavily damaged, and in <a href="/wiki/Groves,_Texas" title="Groves, Texas">Groves</a> (home of "The Texas Pecan Festival"), about the same percentage of pecan trees suffered similar fates. The water treatment plant in <a href="/wiki/Port_Neches,_Texas" title="Port Neches, Texas">Port Neches</a> was heavily damaged. Governor Rick Perry declared a nine-county disaster area as a result of the significant damage in those communities.</p>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Galveston,_Texas" title="Galveston, Texas">Galveston, Texas</a>, parts of the coast not protected by the sea wall experienced minor storm-surge flooding. Distressingly, at the height of Rita\'s landfall, a fire broke out in the <a href="/wiki/Strand_Historic_District" title="Strand Historic District">Strand Historic District</a>; fortunately, the Galveston <a href="/wiki/Fire_department" title="Fire department">fire department</a> reacted quickly, and was able to contain and douse the wind-whipped blaze, limiting damage and preventing the fire from spreading throughout the city. Luckily, no serious injuries were reported, though several historic buildings were either gutted or damaged; a fire-weakened wall of the vacated Yaga\'s Cafe and Bar collapsed several hours later, likely due to lingering wind gusts.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg/220px-Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg/330px-Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg/440px-Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1312" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flooding_in_Galveston_from_Hurricane_Rita.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFloodwaters and destruction left in the aftermath of Hurricane Rita, in an area located near <a href="/wiki/Galveston_Bay" title="Galveston Bay">Galveston Bay</a>, Texas.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>For the most part, <a href="/wiki/Houston,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Houston, Texas">Houston</a> escaped major damage, apart from extensive power interruptions. A few windows blew out of some <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Houston" title="Downtown Houston">downtown</a> skyscrapers, and some trees and traffic signals were downed or damaged.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Thirty-one deaths in <a href="/wiki/Harris_County,_Texas" title="Harris County, Texas">Harris County</a> were attributed to Rita, mostly related to the evacuation and cleanup.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:FellowshipChurch.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/FellowshipChurch.jpg/220px-FellowshipChurch.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/FellowshipChurch.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="225" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:FellowshipChurch.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nChurch in Beaumont with roof ripped off by Hurricane Rita.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>North of Houston, the 2.5-mile-wide (4.0&#160;km) <a href="/wiki/Lake_Livingston" title="Lake Livingston">Lake Livingston</a> <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dam</a> sustained substantial damage from powerful waves driven by wind gusts of up to 117&#160;mph (188&#160;km/h)<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> and officials started an emergency release of water to lessen pressure on the dam. A number of news outlets reported on Sunday, September 25, 2005, that the discharge put lives at risk downstream and threatened a major bridge. Repairs to the dam were expected to take months to complete.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> After water levels were lowered and an inspection was conducted by national and local experts, the dam was declared stable late on Monday, September 26, 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Rita\'s landfall in extreme Southwest Louisiana spared southeast and east Texas far greater damage from storm surge. In particular, Texas\'s coastal communities around <a href="/wiki/Galveston_Bay" title="Galveston Bay">Galveston Bay</a>, located to the west of where the storm came ashore, were largely protected from Rita\'s storm surge by her fortuitous path. However, <a href="/wiki/Sabine_Pass,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Sabine Pass, Texas">Sabine Pass</a> experienced a significant storm surge, which destroyed much of the community. The town was featured on an episode of Extreme Makeover: Home Edition, which built a new fire station in the community. The new fire station was later destroyed in <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Ike" title="Hurricane Ike">Hurricane Ike</a>, which struck the Texas coast in 2008.</p>\n<p>Prudently, a mandatory evacuation of Southeast Texas had been issued before Rita\'s landfall by both local and state governments. As a result of Governor Perry\'s disaster declaration, many residents displaced by, and/or returning home to the aftermath of Rita were able to take advantage of up to 60&#160;days of hotel rooms, generators, chainsaws, and monetary assistance by FEMA.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Elsewhere">Elsewhere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Elsewhere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>As Hurricane Rita passed to the south of Florida on September&#160;20,<sup id="cite_ref-TCR_2-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TCR-2">[2]</a></sup> outer bands to the north produced minor rainfall in parts of southern <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, peaking near 3 inches (76&#160;mm).<sup id="cite_ref-HPC_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HPC-93">[93]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, the storm produced 22 weak tornadoes, mainly rated F0, causing minor isolated damage amounting to roughly $1.2&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC-102">[102]</a></sup> Heavy rains also fell in association with Rita in the state. Most of the western portions of Alabama received more than 3 inches (76&#160;mm), with south-central portions peaking around 7 inches (180&#160;mm).<sup id="cite_ref-HPC_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HPC-93">[93]</a></sup> The remnants of Rita had little impact in <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, only consisting of moderate rainfall, peaking near 5 inches (130&#160;mm).<sup id="cite_ref-HPC_93-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HPC-93">[93]</a></sup> Up to 3 inches (76&#160;mm) of rain fell in southeastern <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a>, most of <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, east and northern <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> before the storm merged with a frontal system on September&#160;26.<sup id="cite_ref-HPC_93-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HPC-93">[93]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The weakened remnants of Hurricane Rita produced heavy rainfall and several tornadoes on September&#160;24 in Arkansas.<sup id="cite_ref-NCDC_102-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NCDC-102">[102]</a></sup> Most of the state received at least 1 inch (25&#160;mm) of rain with maximum amounts around 5 inches (130&#160;mm).<sup id="cite_ref-HPC_93-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HPC-93">[93]</a></sup> Three F2 tornadoes touched down in the state, the first injured five people in <a href="/wiki/Lonoke_County,_Arkansas" title="Lonoke County, Arkansas">Lonoke County</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> the second was a low-end F2 tornado that completely destroyed a double-wide mobile home.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The third was rated as a high-end F2 with winds near 155&#160;mph (249&#160;km/h); it destroyed three structures and severely damaged several others.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Throughout the state, winds gusted up to 50&#160;mph (80&#160;km/h),<sup id="cite_ref-ARKNCDC1_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARKNCDC1-117">[117]</a></sup> leaving 2,976 residences without power.<sup id="cite_ref-PowerRpt4_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PowerRpt4-87">[87]</a></sup> Damage in Arkansas amounted to roughly $1&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-ARKNCDC1_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARKNCDC1-117">[117]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_retired_Atlantic_hurricane_names" title="List of retired Atlantic hurricane names">List of retired Atlantic hurricane names</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement">Retirement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Retirement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Resulting from heavy destruction on the Gulf Coast, the name <i>Rita</i> was retired in the spring of 2006, and will never again be used for an Atlantic hurricane. It was replaced by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Rina_(2011)" class="mw-redirect" title="Hurricane Rina (2011)">Rina</a> for the <a href="/wiki/2011_Atlantic_hurricane_season" title="2011 Atlantic hurricane season">2011 Atlantic hurricane season</a>\'s list.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economic effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png/220px-Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png/330px-Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png/440px-Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png 2x" data-file-width="856" data-file-height="663" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hurricane_Rita_projected_path_September_22.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nProjected path of Hurricane Rita on September&#160;22 highlighting refineries and oil rigs across southeast Texas and southern Louisiana.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The heavy concentration of oil infrastructure in the Gulf of Mexico makes hurricanes of Rita\'s intensity very problematic. Currently, very little spare crude oil capacity exists in the United States, and the Gulf of Mexico produces some 2 million barrels (320,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) per day total, as well as having some 30% of the total refining capacity of the United States. Rita\'s path traveled through a dense area of offshore pipelines and <a href="/wiki/Oil_platform" title="Oil platform">oil platforms</a>, and on land to an area with large refineries. With over half of Gulf production still shut down in the wake of Katrina, some economists have stated that a worst-case scenario is for gasoline prices to briefly touch $5/US gallon ($1.30/L), which would be easily the highest real price for gasoline paid in the United States during the internal combustion era. However, the oil industry escaped essentially unscathed from the storm, and post-storm predictions estimated only minor price rises. With some 200,000 <a href="/wiki/Jobless_claims" title="Jobless claims">jobless claims</a> attributed to Katrina, Rita may have been a further drag on a weakened US economy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>The most pessimistic projections had <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a> growth cut by 1% on an annualized basis in the United States in the second half of 2005, with as many as 500,000 people made <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Unemployment rate">unemployed</a>. Some economists argued that the rebuilding effort could buoy the economy in 2006, while others argued that the energy spike could decrease consumer confidence by enough to send the economy into a full-fledged <a href="/wiki/Recession" title="Recession">recession</a> when combined with the Federal Reserve\'s recent increases in interest rates. While the above did happen, it did not <a href="/wiki/2008_energy_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2008 energy crisis">occur until 2008</a>, nearly three years after Rita\'s impact.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>Due to the impending oil shortage and increasing gas prices, Georgia Governor <a href="/wiki/Sonny_Perdue" title="Sonny Perdue">Sonny Perdue</a> declared what he termed "snow days," closing all Georgia public primary and secondary schools on September 26 and 27 to conserve fuel for buses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>The combined effect of Hurricanes Katrina and Rita was the destruction of an estimated 562 square kilometres (217&#160;sq&#160;mi) of coastal wetlands in Louisiana.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_relief_operations">Military relief operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Military relief operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:JTF_Rita.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/JTF_Rita.jpg/220px-JTF_Rita.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/JTF_Rita.jpg/330px-JTF_Rita.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/JTF_Rita.jpg/440px-JTF_Rita.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="1960" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:JTF_Rita.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSoldiers load hundreds of <a href="/wiki/Meals,_Ready-to-Eat" class="mw-redirect" title="Meals, Ready-to-Eat">Meals, Ready-to-Eat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> onto a <a href="/wiki/CH-47_Chinook" class="mw-redirect" title="CH-47 Chinook">CH-47 Chinook</a> helicopter at <a href="/wiki/Ellington_Field,_Texas" class="mw-redirect" title="Ellington Field, Texas">Ellington Field, Texas</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>On September 24, 2005, following the havoc caused by Hurricanes <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Katrina</a> and Rita, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Guard" class="mw-redirect" title="United States National Guard">National Guard</a> named Brig. Gen. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Douglas_Pritt&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Douglas Pritt (page does not exist)">Douglas Pritt</a> of the <a href="/wiki/41st_Brigade_Combat_Team" class="mw-redirect" title="41st Brigade Combat Team">41st Brigade Combat Team</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oregon_Army_National_Guard" title="Oregon Army National Guard">Oregon Army National Guard</a>, head of Joint Task Force Rita (formally called JTF Ponchartrain).<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> The 1,400 Oregonian soldiers and airmen, including the 1st Battalion of the 186th Infantry which is designated a quick response unit, are joined by engineers and <a href="/wiki/Military_police" title="Military police">military police</a> from Louisiana, the 56th <a href="/wiki/Stryker" title="Stryker">Stryker</a> brigade from <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> and an engineering battalion from <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>. It is their mission to provide relief support for all of the areas in Texas and Louisiana affected by the two storms and to remove obstructions that might otherwise hinder help to those affected.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Red_Cross_operations">American Red Cross operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: American Red Cross operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The American Red Cross continued to provide disaster relief to Hurricane Katrina affected areas, but as a result of Hurricane Rita, had to open additional shelters in other <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="Gulf Coast of the United States">gulf states</a>. The Red Cross also expanded their Hurricane Katrina internet "Safe List" for use by those affected by Hurricane Rita.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="AmeriCorps_relief_operations">AmeriCorps relief operations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hurricane_Rita&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: AmeriCorps relief operations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><a href="/wiki/AmeriCorps" title="AmeriCorps">AmeriCorps</a> sent several crews to Texas, Mississippi and Louisiana in response to <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> and Hurricane Rita. The crews originated from two main organizations, the <a href="/wiki/National_Civilian_Community_Corps" title="National Civilian Community Corps">National Civilian Community Corps</a> (NCCC) and the Washington Conservation Corps (WCC), as well as from smaller Americorps organizations such as Americorps St. Louis\' Emergency Response Team (ERT). The crews performed a number of relief tasks for hurricane survivors, including support on the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Management_Agency" title="Federal Emergency Management Agency">Federal Emergency Management Agency</a> (FEMA)/<a href="/wiki/Carnival_Cruise_Lines" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnival Cruise Lines">Carnival Cruise Lines</a> shelter ship, tarping damaged roofs, and debris removal. As of the beginning of 2006, AmeriCorps teams have been involved in the rebuilding efforts in Louisiana and Mississippi. Teams have also operated volunteer camps like Camp Premier as well as assisted with the Made with Love cafe. As of May 2006, AmeriCorps reported that it would continue to send relief to affected areas.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
